   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Moon landing conspiracy theories
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_Moon_Landing_hoax_theories&amp;redirect=no" title="Apollo Moon Landing hoax theories">
       Apollo Moon Landing hoax theories
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2789" data-file-width="2127" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Crew_During_Training_Exercise_-_GPN-2002-000032.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">
          Astronauts
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">
          Buzz Aldrin
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">
          Neil Armstrong
         </a>
         in NASA's training
         <a href="/wiki/Mockup" title="Mockup">
          mockup
         </a>
         of the Moon and lander module. Conspiracy theorists say that the films of the missions were made using sets similar to this training mockup.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        Moon landing
        <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theory" title="Conspiracy theory">
         conspiracy theories
        </a>
       </b>
       claim that some or all elements of the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
        Apollo program
       </a>
       and the associated
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">
        Moon landings
       </a>
       were
       <a href="/wiki/Hoax" title="Hoax">
        hoaxes
       </a>
       staged by
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       , possibly with the aid of other organizations. The most notable claim is that the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_missions#Manned_Apollo_missions" title="List of Apollo missions">
        six manned landings (1969–72)
       </a>
       were faked and that
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts#Apollo_astronauts_who_walked_on_the_Moon" title="List of Apollo astronauts">
        12 Apollo astronauts
       </a>
       did not actually walk on the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       . Various groups and individuals have made claims since the mid-1970s, that NASA and others knowingly misled the public into believing the landings happened, by manufacturing, tampering with, or destroying evidence including photos,
       <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">
        telemetry
       </a>
       tapes, radio and TV transmissions,
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_rock" title="Moon rock">
        Moon rock
       </a>
       samples, and even killing some key witnesses.
      </p>
      <p>
       Much
       <a href="/wiki/Third-party_evidence_for_Apollo_Moon_landings" title="Third-party evidence for Apollo Moon landings">
        third-party evidence for the landings
       </a>
       exists, and detailed rebuttals to the hoax claims have been made.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the late 2000s, high-definition photos taken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
        Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter
       </a>
       (LRO) of the Apollo landing sites have captured the lander modules and the tracks left by the astronauts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_LRO_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA_LRO-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2012, images were released showing five of the six Apollo missions'
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly" title="Lunar Flag Assembly">
        American flags erected on the Moon
       </a>
       still standing; the exception is that of
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">
        Apollo 11
       </a>
       , which has lain on the lunar surface since being accidentally blown over by the takeoff rocket's exhaust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Conspiracists have managed to sustain public interest in their theories for more than 40 years, despite the rebuttals and third-party evidence. Opinion polls taken in various locations have shown that between 6% and 20% of Americans, 25% of Britons, and 28% of Russians surveyed believe that the manned landings were faked. Even as late as 2001, the
       <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">
        Fox television network
       </a>
       documentary
       <i>
        Conspiracy Theory: Did We Land on the Moon?
       </i>
       claimed NASA faked the first landing in 1969 to win the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">
        Space Race
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greatmoonhoax_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-greatmoonhoax-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

         <a href="#Further_reading">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           11
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Further reading
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33">
         <a href="#External_links">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           12
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           External links
          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34">
           <a href="#Television_specials">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             12.1
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Television specials
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
        Origins
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       An early and influential book about the subject of a moon-landing conspiracy,
       <i>
        We Never Went to the Moon: America's Thirty Billion Dollar Swindle
       </i>
       , was
       <a href="/wiki/Self-publishing" title="Self-publishing">
        self-published
       </a>
       in 1976 by
       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Kaysing" title="Bill Kaysing">
        Bill Kaysing
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite having no knowledge of rockets or technical writing,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Kaysing, a former
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" title="U.S. Navy">
        US Navy
       </a>
       officer with a
       <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">
        Bachelor of Arts
       </a>
       in English, was hired as a senior
       <a href="/wiki/Technical_writer" title="Technical writer">
        technical writer
       </a>
       in 1956 by
       <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne" title="Rocketdyne">
        Rocketdyne
       </a>
       , the company that built the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">
        F-1
       </a>
       engines used on the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">
        Saturn V
       </a>
       rocket.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He served as head of the technical publications unit at the company's Propulsion Field Laboratory until 1963. Kaysing's book made many allegations, and effectively began discussion of the Moon landings being faked.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k-pg7_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-k-pg7-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The book claims that the chance of a successful manned landing on the Moon was calculated to be 0.0017%, and that despite close monitoring by the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        USSR
       </a>
       , it would have been easier for NASA to fake the Moon landings than to really go there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1980, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flat_Earth_Society" title="Flat Earth Society">
        Flat Earth Society
       </a>
       accused NASA of faking the landings, arguing that they were staged by Hollywood with
       <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">
        Walt Disney
       </a>
       sponsorship, based on a script by
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">
        Arthur C. Clarke
       </a>
       and directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">
        Stanley Kubrick
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [Note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Folklorist
       <a href="/wiki/Linda_D%C3%A9gh" title="Linda Dégh">
        Linda Dégh
       </a>
       suggests that writer-director
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hyams" title="Peter Hyams">
        Peter Hyams
       </a>
       ' 1978 film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Capricorn_One" title="Capricorn One">
         Capricorn One
        </a>
       </i>
       , which shows a hoaxed journey to
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       in a
       <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">
        spacecraft
       </a>
       that looks identical to the Apollo craft, might have given a boost to the hoax theory's popularity in the post-
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">
        Vietnam War
       </a>
       era. She notes that this happened during the post-
       <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">
        Watergate
       </a>
       era, when American citizens were
       <a href="/wiki/Credibility_gap" title="Credibility gap">
        inclined to distrust official accounts
       </a>
       . Dégh writes: "The mass media catapult these half-truths into a kind of twilight zone where people can make their guesses sound as truths. Mass media have a terrible impact on people who lack guidance."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-wired-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Man_on_the_Moon" title="A Man on the Moon">
         A Man on the Moon
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       first published in 1994,
       <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Chaikin" title="Andrew Chaikin">
        Andrew Chaikin
       </a>
       mentions that at the time of
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">
        Apollo 8
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">
        lunar-orbit
       </a>
       mission in December 1968,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       similar conspiracy ideas were already in circulation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Conspiracists_and_their_contentions">
        Conspiracists and their contentions
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       After Kaysing, Moon-landing conspiracists include (in alphabetical order):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Allen_(publisher)" title="Marcus Allen (publisher)">
         Marcus Allen
        </a>
        – British publisher of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Nexus_(magazine)" title="Nexus (magazine)">
          Nexus
         </a>
        </i>
        , who said photographs of the lander would not prove that the United States put men on the Moon, and "Getting to the Moon really isn't much of a problem –
        <a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">
         the Russians did that in 1959
        </a>
        . The big problem is getting people there." He suggests that NASA sent robot missions because
        <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">
         radiation
        </a>
        levels in
        <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">
         outer space
        </a>
        would be deadly.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
         <a href="#cite_note-21">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Another variant on this is the idea that NASA and its contractors did not recover quickly enough from the
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
         Apollo 1
        </a>
        fire, and so all the early Apollo missions were faked, with
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">
         Apollos 14
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">
         15
        </a>
        being the first real mission.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
         <a href="#cite_note-22">
          [21]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        William L. Brian – a nuclear engineer who self-published a book in 1982,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Moongate_(book)" title="Moongate (book)">
          Moongate: Suppressed Findings of the U.S. Space Program
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
         <a href="#cite_note-23">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        in which he disputes the Moon's surface gravity.
       </li>
       <li>
        James M. Collier (d. 1998) – American
        <a href="/wiki/Journalist" title="Journalist">
         journalist
        </a>
        and writer, producer of the video
        <i>
         Was It Only a Paper Moon?
        </i>
        (1997).
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Milton_William_Cooper" title="Milton William Cooper">
         Milton William Cooper
        </a>
        (1943–2001) – was an American conspiracy theorist and author. He believed that all of the Apollo missions to the Moon were actually carefully rehearsed productions that were then filmed in large
        <a href="/wiki/Sound_stage" title="Sound stage">
         sound stages
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooper_24-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cooper-24">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/James_H._Fetzer" title="James H. Fetzer">
         James H. Fetzer
        </a>
        – retired American philosophy professor and conspiracy theorist. On May 2, 2013, Dr. Fetzer was interviewed by Dr. Sterling Harwood. Fetzer defended his conspiracist views that all six lunar landings were hoaxes.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpirittoSpirit_25-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-SpirittoSpirit-25">
          [24]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        David Groves – works for Quantech Image Processing. He examined the photo of
        <a href='/wiki/Examination_of_Apollo_Moon_photographs#Apparent_"hot_spots"_in_some_photographs' title="Examination of Apollo Moon photographs">
         Aldrin emerging from the lander
        </a>
        and said he can pinpoint when a spotlight was used. Using the focal length of the
        <a href="/wiki/Camera" title="Camera">
         camera
        </a>
        's lens and an actual boot, he allegedly calculated, using
        <a href="/wiki/Ray_tracing_(graphics)" title="Ray tracing (graphics)">
         ray-tracing
        </a>
        , that the spotlight is between 24 and 36 centimetres (9.4 and 14.2 in) to the right of the camera.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
         <a href="#cite_note-26">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This matches with the sunlit part of Armstrong's
        <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">
         spacesuit
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bootspot_27-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-bootspot-27">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Lewis" title="Clyde Lewis">
         Clyde Lewis
        </a>
        – radio talk show host who says the 1969 moon landing was fake.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
         <a href="#cite_note-28">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clydelewis_29-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-clydelewis-29">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippe_Lheureux" title="Philippe Lheureux">
         Philippe Lheureux
        </a>
        –
        <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">
         French
        </a>
        author of
        <i>
         Lumières sur la Lune
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
        An English language edition was published in 2003,
        <i>
         Moon Landings: Did NASA Lie?
        </i>
        He said that astronauts did land on the Moon, but to stop other states from benefiting from scientific information in the real photos, NASA published fake images.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-31">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yury_Ignatyevich_Mukhin" title="Yury Ignatyevich Mukhin">
         Yury Ignatyevich Mukhin
        </a>
        – Russian opposition politician, publicist and author of the book
        <i>
         Antiapollon: Moonlight scam US
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
         <a href="#cite_note-32">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
        in which he denies all Moon landing evidence and accuses the United States government of plundering the money paid by the American taxpayers for the Moon program. He also claims the
        <a href="/wiki/Central_Committee_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">
         Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union
        </a>
        and some Soviet scientists helped NASA fake the landings.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-33">
          [32]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        David S. Percy – TV producer and member of the
        <a href="/wiki/Royal_Photographic_Society" title="Royal Photographic Society">
         Royal Photographic Society
        </a>
        . He is co-author, along with Mary Bennett, of
        <i>
         Dark Moon: Apollo and the Whistle-Blowers
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and co-producer of
        <i>
         What Happened On the Moon?
        </i>
        He is the main proponent of the '
        <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower" title="Whistleblower">
         whistle-blower
        </a>
        ' accusation, arguing that mistakes in the NASA photos are so obvious that they are evidence that insiders are trying to 'blow the whistle' on the hoax by knowingly adding mistakes that they know will be seen.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
         <a href="#cite_note-35">
          [34]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Most of his allegations of photographic anomalies have been refuted by Doug Millard of the Science Museum in London.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
         <a href="#cite_note-36">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Stanislav Pokrovsky – Russian general director of a scientific-manufacturing enterprise
        <i>
         Project-D-MSK
        </i>
        who calculated that the real speed of the Saturn V rocket at
        <a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">
         S-IC
        </a>
        staging time was only half of what was declared.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
         <a href="#cite_note-37">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
         <a href="#cite_note-38">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
        His analysis appears to assume that the solid rocket plumes from the fuselage and
        <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">
         retrorockets
        </a>
        on the two stages came to an instant halt in the surrounding air so they can be used to estimate the velocity of the rocket. He ignored
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-altitude" title="High-altitude">
         high-altitude
        </a>
        winds and the altitude at staging, 67 km, where air is about 1/10,000 as dense as at
        <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">
         sea level
        </a>
        , and claimed that only a loop around the Moon was possible, not a manned landing on the Moon with return to Earth. He also allegedly found the reason for this – problems with the
        <a href="/wiki/Inconel" title="Inconel">
         Inconel
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Superalloy" title="Superalloy">
         superalloy
        </a>
        used in the F-1 engine.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
         <a href="#cite_note-39">
          [38]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
         <a href="#cite_note-40">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
         <a href="#cite_note-41">
          [40]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Alexander Ivanovich Popov – Russian author of the book
        <i>
         The Americans on the Moon: the great breakthrough or cosmic scam??
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
         <a href="#cite_note-42">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
        in which he aims to prove that Saturn V was in fact a camouflaged
        <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">
         Saturn IB
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
         <a href="#cite_note-43">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and denies all Moon landing evidence.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aron_Ranen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aron Ranen (page does not exist)">
         Aron Ranen
        </a>
        – a
        <a href="/wiki/Filmmaking" title="Filmmaking">
         filmmaker
        </a>
        , directed and produced the documentary
        <i>
         Did We Go?
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-didwego_45-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-didwego-45">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonproject_46-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-moonproject-46">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Ren%C3%A9" title="Ralph René">
         Ralph René
        </a>
        (1933–2008) – was an inventor and 'self-taught' engineering buff. Author of the self-published book
        <i>
         NASA Mooned America!
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Bart_Sibrel" title="Bart Sibrel">
         Bart Sibrel
        </a>
        – a filmmaker, produced and directed four films for his company, AFTH, LLC,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        including a 2001 film,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/A_Funny_Thing_Happened_on_the_Way_to_the_Moon" title="A Funny Thing Happened on the Way to the Moon">
          A Funny Thing Happened on the Way to the Moon
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        examining the evidence of a hoax. The arguments that Sibrel puts forward in this film have been
        <a href="/wiki/Debunker" title="Debunker">
         debunked
        </a>
        by many sources, including Svector Studios' five-part video series
        <i>
         Lunar Legacy
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        which disproves the documentary's main argument that the Apollo crew faked their distance from the Earth while in
        <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">
         low Earth orbit
        </a>
        . Sibrel has said that the effect on the shot covered in his film was made through the use of a transparency of the Earth. Some parts of the original footage, according to Sibrel, were not able to be included on the official releases for the media. On such allegedly censored parts, the correlation between Earth and
        <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">
         Moon phases
        </a>
        can be clearly confirmed, refuting Sibrel's claim that these shots were faked. On September 9, 2002, Sibrel was punched in the face by
        <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">
         Buzz Aldrin
        </a>
        after Sibrel confronted Aldrin with his theories
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and accused the former astronaut of being "a coward, and a liar, and a thief." The
        <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County,_California" title="Los Angeles County, California">
         Los Angeles County
        </a>
        district attorney's office refused to file charges against Aldrin, saying that he had been provoked by Sibrel.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Takahiko_Soejima&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Takahiko Soejima (page does not exist)">
         Takahiko Soejima
        </a>
        <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
         (
         <a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%89%AF%E5%B3%B6%E9%9A%86%E5%BD%A6" title="ja:副島隆彦">
          ja
         </a>
         )
        </span>
        –
        <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">
         Japanese
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">
         political scientist
        </a>
        , author of
        <i>
         人類の月面着陸は無かったろう論
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-54">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Jay Weidner – author and interviewee about the Moon conspiracy theory in the documentary film
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Room_237" title="Room 237">
          Room 237
         </a>
        </i>
        (2012). In this film he repeatedly states that he does not necessarily doubt that America landed men on the Moon even though he thinks there was a conspiracy with Stanley Kubrick to fake the lunar landing footage.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jack_D._White&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jack D. White (page does not exist)">
         Jack D. White
        </a>
        (1927–2012) – was an American photo
        <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">
         historian
        </a>
        known for his attempt to prove forgery in photos and the
        <a href="/wiki/Zapruder_film" title="Zapruder film">
         Zapruder film
        </a>
        related to the
        <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">
         assassination of John F. Kennedy
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nomoregames_55-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nomoregames-55">
          [54]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bugliosi_56-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Bugliosi-56">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
         <a href="#cite_note-57">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitebib_58-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-whitebib-58">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gerhard_Wisnewski&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gerhard Wisnewski (page does not exist)">
         Gerhard Wisnewski
        </a>
        <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
         (
         <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerhard_Wisnewski" title="de:Gerhard Wisnewski">
          de
         </a>
         )
        </span>
        –
        <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">
         German
        </a>
        journalist and filmmaker, author of
        <i>
         One Small Step?: The Great Moon Hoax and the Race to Dominate Earth from Space
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
         <a href="#cite_note-59">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Claimed_motives_of_the_United_States_and_NASA">
        Claimed motives of the United States and NASA
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Those who believe the Moon landings were faked give several theories about the motives of NASA and the
       <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">
        United States government
       </a>
       . The three main theories are below.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Space_Race">
        The Space Race
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Motivation for the United States to engage the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       in a
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">
        Space Race
       </a>
       can be traced to the then on-going
       <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">
        Cold War
       </a>
       . Landing on the Moon was viewed as a national and technological accomplishment that would generate world-wide acclaim. But going to the Moon would be risky and expensive, as exemplified by
       <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">
        President
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy#Space_program" title="John F. Kennedy">
        John F. Kennedy
       </a>
       famously stating in
       <a href="/wiki/We_choose_to_go_to_the_Moon" title="We choose to go to the Moon">
        a 1962 speech
       </a>
       that the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       chose to go
       <i>
        because
       </i>
       it was hard.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Hoax theory-debunker
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_Plait" title="Philip Plait">
        Philip Plait
       </a>
       says in his book
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bad_Astronomy" title="Bad Astronomy">
         Bad Astronomy
        </a>
       </i>
       , that the Soviets – with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_manned_lunar_programs" title="Soviet manned lunar programs">
        their own competing Moon program
       </a>
       , an
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_espionage" title="Nuclear espionage">
        extensive intelligence network
       </a>
       and a formidable scientific community able to analyze NASA data – would have cried foul if the United States tried to fake a Moon landing,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       especially since their own program had failed. Proving a hoax would have been a huge propaganda win for the Soviets. Conspiracist
       <a href="/wiki/Bart_Sibrel" title="Bart Sibrel">
        Bart Sibrel
       </a>
       responded, "the Soviets did not have the capability to track deep space craft until late in 1972, immediately after which, the last three Apollo missions were abruptly canceled."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In fact, the Soviets had been sending
       <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">
        unmanned spacecraft
       </a>
       to the Moon since 1959,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and "during 1962, deep space tracking facilities were introduced at IP-15 in
       <a href="/wiki/Ussuriysk" title="Ussuriysk">
        Ussuriisk
       </a>
       and IP-16 in
       <a href="/wiki/Yevpatoria" title="Yevpatoria">
        Evpatoria
       </a>
       (Crimean Peninsula), while Saturn communication stations were added to IP-3, 4 and 14,"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the latter having a 100 million km range.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Soviet Union tracked the Apollo missions at the Space Transmissions Corps, which was "fully equipped with the latest intelligence-gathering and surveillance equipment."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Mishin" title="Vasily Mishin">
        Vasily Mishin
       </a>
       , in an interview for the article "The Moon Programme That Faltered," describes how the Soviet Moon program dwindled after the Apollo landings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Also, there was nothing "abrupt" about the
       <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Apollo_missions" title="Canceled Apollo missions">
        Apollo cancelations
       </a>
       , which were announced in January and September 1970 for cost-cutting reasons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cancelled_Apollos_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cancelled_Apollos-68">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (See
       <b>
        Vietnam War
       </b>
       below.)
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_funding_and_prestige">
        NASA funding and prestige
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is claimed
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
          <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2014)">
           by whom?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       that NASA faked the landings to avoid humiliation and to ensure that it continued to get funding. NASA raised "about US$30 billion" to go to the Moon, and
       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Kaysing" title="Bill Kaysing">
        Kaysing
       </a>
       claimed in his book that this could have been used to "pay off" many people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since most conspiracists believe that sending men to the Moon was impossible at the time,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       they argue that landings had to be faked to fulfill Kennedy's 1961 goal, "before this decade is out, of landing a man on the Moon and returning him safely to the Earth."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Special_Message_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Special_Message-71">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, NASA accounted for the cost of Apollo to the US Congress in 1973, totaling US$25.4 billion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mary Bennett and David Percy have claimed in
       <i>
        Dark Moon: Apollo and the Whistle-Blowers
       </i>
       , that, with all the known and unknown hazards,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       NASA would not risk broadcasting an astronaut getting sick or dying on live television.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The counter-argument generally given is that NASA in fact
       <i>
        did
       </i>
       incur a great deal of public humiliation and potential political opposition to the program by losing an entire crew in the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
        Apollo 1
       </a>
       fire during a ground test, leading to its upper management team being questioned by Senate and House of Representatives space oversight committees.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There was in fact no video broadcast during either the landing or takeoff because of technological limitations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam_War">
        Vietnam War
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The American Patriot Friends Network claimed in 2009 that the landings helped the United States government distract public attention from the unpopular
       <a href="/wiki/Role_of_the_United_States_in_the_Vietnam_War#Americanization" title="Role of the United States in the Vietnam War">
        Vietnam War
       </a>
       , and so manned landings suddenly ended about the same time that the United States ended its involvement in the war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, the ending of the landings was not "sudden" (see
       <b>
        The Space Race
       </b>
       above). The war was one of several federal budget items with which NASA had to compete; NASA's budget peaked in 1966, and fell by 42.3% by 1972.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was the reason the final flights were cut, along with plans for even more ambitious follow-on programs such as a permanent
       <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">
        space station
       </a>
       and manned flight to Mars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hoax_claims_and_rebuttals">
        Hoax claims and rebuttals
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Many Moon-landing conspiracy theories have been put forward: claiming either that the landings did not happen and that NASA employees have lied, or that the landings did happen but not in the way that has been told. Conspiracists have focused on perceived gaps or inconsistencies in the historical record of the missions. The foremost idea is that the whole manned landing program was a hoax from start to end. Some claim that the technology to send men to the Moon was lacking or that the
       <a href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt" title="Van Allen radiation belt">
        Van Allen radiation belts
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">
        solar flares
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">
        coronal mass ejections
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">
        cosmic rays
       </a>
       made such a trip impossible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k-pg7_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-k-pg7-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Vince Calder and Andrew Johnson, scientists from
       <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">
        Argonne National Laboratory
       </a>
       , gave detailed answers to the conspiracists' claims on the laboratory's website.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They show that NASA's portrayal of the Moon landing is fundamentally accurate, allowing for such common mistakes as mislabeled photos and imperfect personal recollections. Using the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_process" title="Scientific process">
        scientific process
       </a>
       , any hypothesis that is contradicted by the observable facts may be rejected. The 'real landing' hypothesis is a single story since it comes from a single source, but there is no unity in the hoax hypothesis because hoax accounts vary between conspiracists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_conspirators_involved">
        Number of conspirators involved
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/James_Longuski" title="James Longuski">
        James Longuski
       </a>
       (Professor of Aeronautics and Astronautics Engineering at
       <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">
        Purdue University
       </a>
       ), the conspiracy theories are impossible because of their size and complexity. The conspiracy would have to involve more than 400,000 people who worked on the Apollo project for nearly ten years, the 12 men who walked on the Moon, the six others who flew with them as
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
        Command Module
       </a>
       pilots, and another six astronauts who orbited the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [Note 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hundreds of thousands of people – including astronauts, scientists, engineers, technicians, and skilled laborers – would have had to keep the secret. Longuski argues that it would have been much easier to really land on the Moon than to generate such a huge conspiracy to fake the landings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To date, nobody from the United States government or NASA who would have had a link to the Apollo program has said the Moon landings were hoaxes.
       <a href="/wiki/Penn_Jillette" title="Penn Jillette">
        Penn Jillette
       </a>
       made note of this in the "Conspiracy Theories" episode of his contrarian television show
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Penn_%26_Teller:_Bullshit!" title="Penn &amp; Teller: Bullshit!">
         Penn &amp; Teller: Bullshit!
        </a>
       </i>
       in 2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bullshit_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bullshit-85">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With the number of people involved, and noting the
       <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">
        Watergate scandal
       </a>
       , Jillette noted that
       <i>
        someone
       </i>
       would have outed the hoax by now.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Photograph_and_film_oddities">
        Photograph and film oddities
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Examination_of_Apollo_Moon_photographs" title="Examination of Apollo Moon photographs">
        Examination of Apollo Moon photographs
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Moon-landing conspiracists focus heavily on NASA photos. They point to oddities in photos and films taken on the Moon. Photography experts (including those unrelated to NASA) answer that the oddities are what one would expect from a real Moon landing, and not what would happen with tweaked or studio imagery. Some of the main arguments and counter-arguments are listed below.
      </p>
      <p>
       1. In some photos,
       <a href="/wiki/Reticle" title="Reticle">
        crosshairs
       </a>
       seem to be behind objects. The cameras were fitted with a
       <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9seau_plate" title="Réseau plate">
        Réseau plate
       </a>
       (a clear glass plate with crosshairs etched on), making it impossible for any photographed object to appear "in front" of the grid. This suggests that objects have been "pasted" over them.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           This only appears in copied and scanned photos, not the originals. It is caused by overexposure: the bright white areas of the emulsion "bleed" over the thin black crosshairs. The crosshairs are only about 0.004 inches thick (0.1 mm) and emulsion would only have to bleed about half that much to fully obscure it. Furthermore, there are many photos where the middle of the crosshair is "washed-out" but the rest is intact. In some photos of the American flag, parts of one crosshair appear on the red stripes, but parts of the same crosshair are faded or invisible on the white stripes. There would have been no reason to "paste" white stripes onto the flag.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clavius-crosshairs_86-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-clavius-crosshairs-86">
             [84]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <center>
       <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">
        <tr>
         <td>
          <table style="width:220px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">

               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">

              </div>

            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">

             <div class="gallerytext" style="min-height:6.1em;width:207px;line-height:1.3em;padding:2px 6px 1px 6px;margin:0px;border:none;border-width:0px">

             </div>

             <div class="center">
              <div class="floatnone">
               <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scott_Gives_Salute_-_GPN-2000-001114.jpg" title="David Scott salutes the American flag during the Apollo 15 mission. The arms of the crosshair are washed-out on the white stripes of the flag (Photo ID: AS15-88-11863)">

               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">
            <td style="display:block;font-size:94%;padding:0px">
             <div class="gallerytext" style="min-height:6.1em;width:207px;line-height:1.3em;padding:2px 6px 1px 6px;margin:0px;border:none;border-width:0px">
              <a href="/wiki/David_Scott" title="David Scott">
               David Scott
              </a>
              salutes the American flag during the
              <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">
               Apollo 15
              </a>
              mission. The arms of the crosshair are washed-out on the white stripes of the flag (Photo ID: AS15-88-11863)
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
          </table>
          <table style="width:220px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">
           <tr>
            <td style="height:200px;border:1px solid #CCCCCC;background-color:#F8F8F8;padding:0px;text-align:center">
             <div class="center">
              <div class="floatnone">
               <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_flag_crop.jpg" title="Close-up of the flag, showing washed-out crosshairs">
                <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="231" data-file-width="259" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Apollo_15_flag_crop.jpg/200px-Apollo_15_flag_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Apollo_15_flag_crop.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/>
               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">
            <td style="display:block;font-size:94%;padding:0px">
             <div class="gallerytext" style="min-height:6.1em;width:207px;line-height:1.3em;padding:2px 6px 1px 6px;margin:0px;border:none;border-width:0px">
              Close-up of the flag, showing washed-out crosshairs
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
          </table>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </center>
      <p>
       2. Crosshairs are sometimes rotated or in the wrong place.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           This is a result of popular photos being cropped and/or rotated for aesthetic impact.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clavius-crosshairs_86-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-clavius-crosshairs-86">
            [84]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       3. The quality of the photographs is implausibly high.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           There are many poor-quality photos taken by the Apollo astronauts. NASA chose to publish only the best examples.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clavius-crosshairs_86-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-clavius-crosshairs-86">
            [84]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
           <a href="#cite_note-87">
            [85]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           The Apollo astronauts used high-resolution
           <a href="/wiki/Hasselblad" title="Hasselblad">
            Hasselblad
           </a>
           500 EL cameras with
           <a href="/wiki/Carl_Zeiss_AG" title="Carl Zeiss AG">
            Carl Zeiss optics
           </a>
           and a 70 mm
           <a href="/wiki/Medium_format_(film)" title="Medium format (film)">
            medium format
           </a>
           film magazine.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
           <a href="#cite_note-88">
            [86]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
           <a href="#cite_note-89">
            [87]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       4. There are no
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       in any of the photos; the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">
        Apollo 11
       </a>
       astronauts also claimed in a post-mission press conference to not remember seeing any stars.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The astronauts were talking about naked-eye sightings of stars during the lunar daytime. They regularly sighted stars through the spacecraft navigation optics while aligning their inertial reference platforms, the
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_PGNCS" title="Apollo PGNCS">
            Apollo PGNCS
           </a>
           .
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
           <a href="#cite_note-90">
            [88]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           All manned landings happened during the lunar daytime. Thus, the stars were outshone by the sun and by sunlight reflected off the Moon's surface. The astronauts' eyes were adapted to the sunlit landscape around them so that they could not see the relatively faint stars. Likewise, cameras were set for daylight exposure and could not detect the stars.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
            <a href="#cite_note-91">
             [89]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
            <a href="#cite_note-92">
             [90]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Camera settings can turn a well-lit background to black when the foreground object is brightly lit, forcing the camera to increase shutter speed so that the foreground light does not wash out the image. A demonstration of this effect is here.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
            <a href="#cite_note-93">
             [91]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The effect is similar to not being able to see stars from a brightly lit car park at night – the stars only become visible when the lights are turned off. The astronauts could see stars with the naked eye only when they were in the shadow of the Moon.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
           <a href="#cite_note-94">
            [92]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
           <a href="#cite_note-95">
            [93]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           A special far
           <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
            ultraviolet
           </a>
           camera, the
           <a href="/wiki/Far_Ultraviolet_Camera/Spectrograph" title="Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph">
            Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph
           </a>
           , was taken to the lunar surface on
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16">
            Apollo 16
           </a>
           and operated in the shadow of the
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
            Apollo Lunar Module
           </a>
           (LM). It took photos of Earth and of many stars, some of which are dim in visible light but bright in the ultraviolet. These observations were later matched with observations taken by orbiting ultraviolet telescopes. Furthermore, the positions of those stars with respect to Earth are correct for the time and location of the Apollo 16 photos.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
           <a href="#cite_note-96">
            [94]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
           <a href="#cite_note-97">
            [95]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Photos of the solar corona that included the planet
           <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
            Mercury
           </a>
           and some
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Background_stars" title="Background stars">
            background stars
           </a>
           were taken from lunar orbit by Apollo 15 Command Module Pilot
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Worden" title="Al Worden">
            Al Worden
           </a>
          </i>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
           <a href="#cite_note-98">
            [96]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Photos of the planet
           <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
            Venus
           </a>
           (which is much brighter than any of the stars) were taken from the Moon's surface by astronaut
           <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">
            Alan Shepard
           </a>
           during the Apollo 14 mission.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venusovera14_99-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-venusovera14-99">
            [97]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <center>
       <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">
        <tr>

       </table>
      </center>
      <p>
       5. The angle and color of shadows are inconsistent. This suggests that artificial lights were used.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Shadows on the Moon are complicated by reflected light, uneven ground,
           <a href="/wiki/Wide-angle_lens" title="Wide-angle lens">
            wide-angle lens
           </a>
           distortion, and
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_dust" title="Lunar dust">
            lunar dust
           </a>
           . There are several light sources: the Sun, sunlight reflected from the Earth, sunlight reflected from the Moon's surface, and sunlight reflected from the astronauts and the Lunar Module. Light from these sources is scattered by lunar dust in many directions, including into shadows. Shadows falling into craters and hills may appear longer, shorter and distorted.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
            <a href="#cite_note-100">
             [98]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Furthermore, shadows display the properties of
           <a href="/wiki/Vanishing_point" title="Vanishing point">
            vanishing point
           </a>
           perspective, leading them to converge to a point on the horizon.
          </i>
         </li>
         <li>
          This theory was debunked on the
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/MythBusters" title="MythBusters">
            MythBusters
           </a>
          </i>
          episode "
          <a href='/wiki/MythBusters_(2008_season)#Episode_104_–_"NASA_Moon_Landing"' title="MythBusters (2008 season)">
           NASA Moon Landing
          </a>
          ".
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       6. There are identical backgrounds in photos which, according to their captions, were taken miles apart. This suggests that a painted background was used.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Backgrounds were not identical, just similar. What appears as nearby hills in some photos are actually mountains many miles away. On Earth, objects that are further away will appear fainter and less detailed. On the Moon, there is no
           <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
            atmosphere
           </a>
           or
           <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">
            haze
           </a>
           to obscure faraway objects, thus they appear clearer and nearer.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
            <a href="#cite_note-101">
             [99]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Furthermore, there are very few objects (such as trees) to help judge distance. One case is debunked in "Who Mourns For Apollo?" by Mike Bara.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whomourns_102-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-whomourns-102">
             [100]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       7. The number of photos taken is implausibly high. Up to one photo per 50 seconds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Simplified gear with fixed settings allowed two photos a second. Many were taken immediately after each other as stereo pairs or panorama sequences. The calculation (one per 50 seconds) was based on a lone astronaut on the surface, and does not take into account that there were two astronauts sharing the workload during an
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">
            Extra-vehicular activity
           </a>
           (EVA).
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       8. The photos contain artifacts like the two seemingly matching 'C's on a rock and on the ground. These may be labeled studio props.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The "C"-shaped objects are most likely printing imperfections and do not appear in the original film from the camera. It has been suggested that the "C" is a coiled hair.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whomourns_102-1">
            <a href="#cite_note-whomourns-102">
             [100]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
            <a href="#cite_note-104">
             [102]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>

             </div>

             <div class="center">
              <div class="floatnone">
               <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo16CRock.jpg" title="Close-up of later generation prints of AS16-107-17446">

              Close-up of later generation prints of AS16-107-17446
             </div>
            </td>

          </table>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </center>
      <p>
       9. A resident of
       <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">
        Perth, Western Australia
       </a>
       , a woman named Una Ronald (a pseudonym created by the authors of the source
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), said that for two or three seconds she saw a
       <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">
        Coca-Cola
       </a>
       bottle roll across the lower right quadrant of her television screen that was displaying the live broadcast of the Apollo 11 EVA. She also said that several letters appeared in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_West_Australian" title="The West Australian">
         The West Australian
        </a>
       </i>
       discussing the Coca-Cola bottle incident within ten days of the lunar landing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           No such newspaper reports or recordings have been found.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cokebottle_107-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-cokebottle-107">
             [105]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Ronald's claims have only been relayed by one source.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-1">
            <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34">
             [33]
            </a>
           </sup>
           There are also flaws in the story, e.g. the statement that she had to stay up late to watch the Moon landing live is easily discounted by many witnesses in Australia who watched the landing in the middle of their daytime.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
            <a href="#cite_note-108">
             [106]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
            <a href="#cite_note-109">
             [107]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       10. The book
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Moon_Shot" title="Moon Shot">
         Moon Shot
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       contains an obvious composite photo of Alan Shepard hitting a golf ball on the Moon with another astronaut.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           It was used instead of the only existing real images, from the TV monitor, which the editors seemingly felt were too grainy for their book. The book publishers did not work for NASA.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       11. There appear to be "hot spots" in some photos that look like a huge spotlight was used.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Pits on the Moon's surface focus and reflect light like the tiny glass spheres used in the coating of street signs, or dewdrops on wet grass. This creates a glow around the photographer's own shadow when it appears in a photograph (see
           <a href="/wiki/Heiligenschein" title="Heiligenschein">
            Heiligenschein
           </a>
           ).
          </i>
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           If the astronaut is standing in sunlight while photographing into shade, light reflected off his white spacesuit yields a similar effect to a spotlight.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bootspot_27-1">
            <a href="#cite_note-bootspot-27">
             [26]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           Some widely published Apollo photos were high-contrast copies. Scans of the original transparencies are generally much more evenly lit. An example is shown below:
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <center>
       <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">
        <tr>
         <td>
          <table style="width:220px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">

             <div class="center">
              <div class="floatnone">
               <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg" title="Original photo of Buzz Aldrin during Apollo 11">
                <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="3936" data-file-width="3912" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg/179px-Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg/268px-Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg/358px-Aldrin_Apollo_11_original.jpg 2x" width="179"/>
               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>

              <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">

              </a>

               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
