             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
          </table>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </center>
      <p>
       12. Who filmed Neil Armstrong stepping onto the Moon?
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The Lunar Module did. While still on the steps, Armstrong deployed the Modularized Equipment Stowage Assembly from the side of the Lunar Module. This housed, amongst other things, the TV camera. This meant that upward of 600 million people on Earth could watch the live feed.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">
        Environment
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       1. The astronauts could not have survived the trip because of exposure to radiation from the
       <a href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt" title="Van Allen radiation belt">
        Van Allen radiation belt
       </a>
       and galactic ambient radiation (see
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">
        radiation poisoning
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">
        health threat from cosmic rays
       </a>
       ). Some conspiracists have suggested that
       <a href="/wiki/Starfish_Prime" title="Starfish Prime">
        Starfish Prime
       </a>
       (a
       <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_nuclear_explosion" title="High-altitude nuclear explosion">
        high-altitude nuclear test
       </a>
       in 1962) was a failed attempt to disrupt the Van Allen belts.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           There are two main Van Allen belts – the inner belt and the outer belt – and a transient third belt.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
            <a href="#cite_note-111">
             [109]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The inner belt is the more dangerous one, containing energetic protons. The outer one has less-dangerous low-energy electrons (
           <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">
            Beta particles
           </a>
           ).
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanallenbelts_112-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-vanallenbelts-112">
             [110]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods109_113-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-woods109-113">
             [111]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The Apollo spacecraft passed through the inner belt in a matter of minutes and the outer belt in about ​
           <span class="frac nowrap">
            1
            <span class="visualhide">
            </span>
            <sup>
             1
            </sup>
            ⁄
            <sub>
             2
            </sub>
           </span>
           hours.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods109_113-1">
            <a href="#cite_note-woods109-113">
             [111]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The astronauts were shielded from the ionizing radiation by the aluminum hulls of the spacecraft.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods109_113-2">
            <a href="#cite_note-woods109-113">
             [111]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plait162_114-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-plait162-114">
             [112]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Furthermore, the orbital transfer trajectory from Earth to the Moon through the belts was chosen to lessen radiation exposure.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plait162_114-1">
            <a href="#cite_note-plait162-114">
             [112]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Even Dr.
           <a href="/wiki/James_Van_Allen" title="James Van Allen">
            James Van Allen
           </a>
           , the discoverer of the Van Allen radiation belts, rebutted the claims that radiation levels were too harmful for the Apollo missions.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
            <a href="#cite_note-115">
             [113]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Plait cited an average dose of less than 1
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%B6ntgen_equivalent_man" title="Röntgen equivalent man">
            rem
           </a>
           (10
           <a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">
            mSv
           </a>
           ), which is equivalent to the ambient radiation received by living at sea level for three years.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
            <a href="#cite_note-116">
             [114]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The total radiation received on the trip was about the same as allowed for workers in the nuclear energy field for a year
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods109_113-3">
           <a href="#cite_note-woods109-113">
            [111]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
           <a href="#cite_note-117">
            [115]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <i>
           and not much more than what Space Shuttle astronauts received.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanallenbelts_112-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-vanallenbelts-112">
            [110]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       2. Film in the cameras would have been fogged by this radiation.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The film was kept in metal containers that stopped radiation from fogging the film's emulsion.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
            <a href="#cite_note-118">
             [116]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Furthermore, film carried by unmanned lunar probes such as the
           <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbiter_program" title="Lunar Orbiter program">
            Lunar Orbiter
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Luna_3" title="Luna 3">
            Luna 3
           </a>
           (which used on-board film development processes) was not fogged.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       3. The Moon's surface during the daytime is so hot that camera film would have melted.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           There is no atmosphere to efficiently bind lunar surface heat to devices (such as cameras) that are not in direct contact with it. In a
           <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
            vacuum
           </a>
           , only radiation remains as a heat transfer mechanism. The physics of radiative heat transfer are thoroughly understood, and the proper use of passive optical coatings and paints was enough to control the temperature of the film within the cameras; Lunar Module temperatures were controlled with similar coatings that gave them a gold color. Also, while the Moon's surface does get very hot at lunar noon, every Apollo landing was made shortly after lunar sunrise at the landing site; the
           <a href="/wiki/Lunar_day" title="Lunar day">
            Moon's day
           </a>
           is about 29½ Earth days long, meaning that one Moon day (dawn to dusk) lasts nearly fifteen Earth days. During the longer stays, the astronauts did notice increased cooling loads on their spacesuits as the sun and surface temperature continued to rise, but the effect was easily countered by the passive and active cooling systems.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
            <a href="#cite_note-119">
             [117]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The film was not in direct sunlight, so it was not overheated.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
           <a href="#cite_note-120">
            [118]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       4. The Apollo 16 crew could not have survived a big
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">
        solar flare
       </a>
       firing out when they were on their way to the Moon.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           No large solar flare occurred during the flight of Apollo 16. There were large solar flares in August 1972, after Apollo 16 returned to Earth and before the flight of
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
            Apollo 17
           </a>
           .
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
            <a href="#cite_note-121">
             [119]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
            <a href="#cite_note-122">
             [120]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       5. The
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly" title="Lunar Flag Assembly">
        flag
       </a>
       placed on the surface by the astronauts fluttered despite there being no wind on the Moon. This suggests that it was filmed on Earth and a breeze caused the flag to flutter. Sibrel said that it may have been caused by indoor fans used to cool the astronauts since their spacesuit cooling systems would have been too heavy on Earth.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The flag was fastened to a
          </i>
          Г-
          <i>
           shaped rod (see
           <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly" title="Lunar Flag Assembly">
            Lunar Flag Assembly
           </a>
           ) so that it did not hang down. The flag only seemed to flutter when the astronauts were moving it into position. Without air drag, these movements caused the free corner of the flag to swing like a
           <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">
            pendulum
           </a>
           for some time. The flag was rippled because it had been folded during storage – the ripples could be mistaken for movement in a still photo. Videos show that when the astronauts let go of the flagpole it vibrates briefly but then remains still
          </i>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
           <a href="#cite_note-123">
            [121]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
           <a href="#cite_note-124">
            [122]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
           <a href="#cite_note-125">
            [123]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          This theory was debunked on the
          <i>
           MythBusters
          </i>
          episode "
          <a href='/wiki/MythBusters_(2008_season)#Episode_104_–_"NASA_Moon_Landing"' title="MythBusters (2008 season)">
           NASA Moon Landing
          </a>
          ".
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <center>
       <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">

               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">
            <td style="display:block;font-size:94%;padding:0px">
             <div class="gallerytext" style="min-height:6.1em;width:207px;line-height:1.3em;padding:2px 6px 1px 6px;margin:0px;border:none;border-width:0px">

              <a href="/wiki/Camera" title="Camera">
               camera
              </a>
              , the flag is unchanged
             </div>

           </tr>
          </table>
          <table style="width:220px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">

             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">
            <td style="display:block;font-size:94%;padding:0px">
             <div class="gallerytext" style="min-height:6.1em;width:207px;line-height:1.3em;padding:2px 6px 1px 6px;margin:0px;border:none;border-width:0px">
              Animation of the two photos, showing that though Armstrong's camera moved between exposures, the flag is not waving
             </div>
            </td>
           </tr>
          </table>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </center>
      <p>
       6. Footprints in the Moondust are unexpectedly well preserved, despite the lack of moisture.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The Moon dust has not been weathered like Earth sand and has sharp edges. This allows the Moondust particles to stick together and hold their shape in the vacuum. The astronauts likened it to "talcum powder or wet sand".
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whomourns_102-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-whomourns-102">
            [100]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          This theory was debunked on the
          <i>
           MythBusters
          </i>
          episode "
          <a href='/wiki/MythBusters_(2008_season)#Episode_104_–_"NASA_Moon_Landing"' title="MythBusters (2008 season)">
           NASA Moon Landing
          </a>
          ".
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       7. The alleged Moon landings used either a sound stage or were filmed outside in a remote desert with the astronauts either using harnesses or slow-motion photography to make it look like they were on the Moon.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           While the
           <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">
            HBO
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">
            miniseries
           </a>
           "
           <a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon_(miniseries)" title="From the Earth to the Moon (miniseries)">
            From the Earth to the Moon
           </a>
           ", and a scene from the movie "
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13_(film)" title="Apollo 13 (film)">
            Apollo 13
           </a>
           " used the sound-stage and harness setup, it is clearly seen from those films that when dust rose it did not quickly settle; some dust briefly formed clouds. In the film footage from the Apollo missions, dust kicked up by the astronauts' boots and the wheels of the
           <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">
            Lunar Roving Vehicles
           </a>
           rose quite high due to the lower lunar gravity, and settled quickly to the ground in an uninterrupted parabolic arc since there was no air to suspend the dust. Even if there had been a sound stage for hoax Moon landings that had the air pumped out, the dust would have reached nowhere near the height and trajectory as in the Apollo film footage because of Earth's greater gravity.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           During the Apollo 15 mission,
           <a href="/wiki/David_Scott" title="David Scott">
            David Scott
           </a>
           did an experiment by dropping a hammer and a falcon feather at the same time. Both fell at the same rate and hit the ground at the same time. This proved that he was in a vacuum.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
            <a href="#cite_note-126">
             [124]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          This theory was debunked on the
          <i>
           MythBusters
          </i>
          episode "
          <a href='/wiki/MythBusters_(2008_season)#Episode_104_–_"NASA_Moon_Landing"' title="MythBusters (2008 season)">
           NASA Moon Landing
          </a>
          ".
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <center>
       <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em">
        <tr>
         <td>
          <table style="width:220px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">
           <tr>
            <td style="height:200px;border:1px solid #CCCCCC;background-color:#F8F8F8;padding:0px;text-align:center">
             <div class="center">
              <div class="floatnone">

      </center>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_issues">
        Mechanical issues
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11_under_LM.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2373" data-file-width="2349" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo11_under_LM.jpg/220px-Apollo11_under_LM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo11_under_LM.jpg/330px-Apollo11_under_LM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo11_under_LM.jpg/440px-Apollo11_under_LM.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11_under_LM.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Under the Apollo 11 Lunar Module
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       1. The Lunar Modules made no blast
       <a href="/wiki/Explosion_crater" title="Explosion crater">
        craters
       </a>
       or any sign of dust scatter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           No crater should be expected. The 10,000-pound thrust
           <a href="/wiki/Descent_Propulsion_System" title="Descent Propulsion System">
            Descent Propulsion System
           </a>
           was throttled very far down during the final landing.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
            <a href="#cite_note-128">
             [126]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The Lunar Module was no longer quickly decelerating, so the descent engine only had to support the lander's own weight, which was lessened by the Moon's gravity and by the near exhaustion of the descent propellants. At landing, the engine thrust divided by the nozzle exit area is only about 10
           <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">
            kilopascals
           </a>
           (1.5
           <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">
            PSI
           </a>
           ).
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
            <a href="#cite_note-129">
             [127]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Beyond the engine nozzle, the plume spreads, and the pressure drops very quickly. Rocket exhaust gasses expand much more quickly after leaving the engine nozzle in a vacuum than in an atmosphere. The effect of an atmosphere on rocket plumes can be easily seen in launches from Earth; as the rocket rises through the thinning atmosphere, the exhaust plumes broaden very noticeably. To lessen this, rocket engines made for vacuums have longer bells than those made for use on Earth, but they still cannot stop this spreading. The lander's exhaust gasses, therefore, expanded quickly well beyond the landing site. The descent engines
           <u>
            did
           </u>
           scatter a lot of very fine surface dust as seen in
           <a href="/wiki/16_mm_film" title="16 mm film">
            16mm movies
           </a>
           of each landing, and many mission commanders spoke of its effect on visibility. The landers were generally moving horizontally as well as vertically, and photos do show scouring of the surface along the final descent path. Finally, the lunar
           <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
            regolith
           </a>
           is very compact below its surface dust layer, making it impossible for the descent engine to blast out a "crater".
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ba-pg163-165_130-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-ba-pg163-165-130">
             [128]
            </a>
           </sup>
           A blast crater was measured under the Apollo 11 lander using shadow lengths of the descent engine bell and estimates of the amount that the landing gear had compressed and how deep the lander footpads had pressed into the lunar surface and it was found that the engine had eroded between 4 and 6 inches of regolith out from underneath the engine bell during the final descent and landing.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-as11psr_131-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-as11psr-131">
            [129]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       2. The second stage of the launch rocket and/or the Lunar Module
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module#Ascent_stage" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
        ascent stage
       </a>
       made no visible flame.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The Lunar Modules used
           <a href="/wiki/Aerozine_50" title="Aerozine 50">
            Aerozine 50
           </a>
           (fuel) and
           <a href="/wiki/Dinitrogen_tetroxide" title="Dinitrogen tetroxide">
            dinitrogen tetroxide
           </a>
           (oxidizer) propellants, chosen for simplicity and reliability; they ignite
           <a href="/wiki/Hypergolic_propellant" title="Hypergolic propellant">
            hypergolically
           </a>
           – upon contact – without the need for a spark. These propellants produce a nearly
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparent_materials" title="Transparent materials">
            transparent
           </a>
           exhaust.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
            <a href="#cite_note-132">
             [130]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The same fuel was used by the core of the American
           <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">
            Titan II
           </a>
           rocket. The transparency of their plumes is apparent in many launch photos. The plumes of rocket engines fired in a vacuum spread out very quickly as they leave the engine nozzle (see above), further lessening their visibility. Finally, rocket engines often run "rich" to slow internal corrosion. On Earth, the excess fuel burns in contact with atmospheric oxygen, enhancing the visible flame. This cannot happen in a vacuum.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>

       <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

         </div>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </center>
      <p>
       3. The Lunar Modules weighed 17 tons and made no mark on the Moondust, yet footprints can be seen beside them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           On the surface of the Earth, Apollo 11's fueled and crewed Lunar Module, Eagle, would have weighed approximately 17
           <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">
            short tons
           </a>
           (15,300 kg). On the surface of the Moon, however, after expending fuel and oxidizer on her descent from lunar orbit, the lander weighed about 2,698 pounds (1,224 kg).
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
            <a href="#cite_note-134">
             [132]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The astronauts were much lighter than the lander, but their boots were much smaller than the lander's approximately 3-foot (91 cm) diameter footpads.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
            <a href="#cite_note-135">
             [133]
            </a>
           </sup>
           Pressure (or force per unit area) rather than mass determines the amount of regolith compression. In some photos, the footpads
           <u>
            did
           </u>
           press into the regolith, especially when they moved sideways at touchdown. (The bearing pressure under Apollo 11's footpads, with the lander being about 44 times the weight of an EVA-configured astronaut, would have been of similar magnitude to the bearing pressure exerted by the astronauts' boots.)
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
           <a href="#cite_note-136">
            [134]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       4. The air conditioning units that were part of the astronauts' spacesuits could not have worked in an environment of no atmosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooper_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cooper-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The cooling units could
           <u>
            only
           </u>
           work in a vacuum. Water from a tank in the backpack flowed out through tiny pores in a metal
           <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">
            sublimator
           </a>
           plate where it quickly vaporized into space. The loss of the heat of vaporization froze the remaining water, forming a layer of ice on the outside of the plate that also sublimated into space (turning from a solid directly into a gas). A separate water loop flowed through the LCG (
           <a href="/wiki/Liquid_Cooling_and_Ventilation_Garment" title="Liquid Cooling and Ventilation Garment">
            Liquid Cooling Garment
           </a>
           ) worn by the astronaut, carrying his metabolic waste heat through the sublimator plate where it was cooled and returned to the LCG. Twelve pounds (5.4 kg) of feedwater gave about eight hours of cooling; because of its bulk, it was often the limiting consumable on the length of an EVA.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2627" data-file-width="3000" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg/220px-Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg/330px-Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg/440px-Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_3" title="Surveyor 3">
          Surveyor 3
         </a>
         with
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">
          Apollo 12
         </a>
         lander in background
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transmissions">
        Transmissions
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       1. There should have been more than a two-second delay in communications between Earth and the Moon, at a distance of 400,000 km (250,000 mi).
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The round trip light travel time of more than two seconds is apparent in all the real-time recordings of the lunar audio, but this does not always appear as expected. There may also be some documentary films where the delay has been edited out. Reasons for editing the audio may be time constraints or in the interest of clarity.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
           <a href="#cite_note-137">
            [135]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       2. Typical delays in communication were about 0.5 seconds.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           Claims that the delays were only half a second are untrue, as examination of the original recordings shows. Also, there should not be a consistent time delay between every response, as the conversation is being recorded at one end –
           <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">
            Mission Control
           </a>
           . Responses from Mission Control could be heard without any delay, as the recording is being made at the same time that Houston receives the transmission from the Moon.
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       3. The
       <a href="/wiki/Parkes_Observatory" title="Parkes Observatory">
        Parkes Observatory
       </a>
       in Australia was billed to the world for weeks as the site that would be relaying communications from the first moonwalk. However, five hours before transmission they were told to stand down.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           The timing of the first moonwalk was changed after the landing. In fact, delays in getting the moonwalk started meant that Parkes did cover almost the entire Apollo 11 moonwalk.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
           <a href="#cite_note-138">
            [136]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       4. Parkes supposedly had the clearest video feed from the Moon, but Australian media and all other known sources ran a live feed from the United States.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           While that was the original plan, and, according to some sources, the official policy, the Australian Broadcasting Commission (ABC) did take the transmission direct from the Parkes and
           <a href="/wiki/Honeysuckle_Creek_Tracking_Station" title="Honeysuckle Creek Tracking Station">
            Honeysuckle Creek
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">
            radio telescopes
           </a>
           . These were converted to
           <a href="/wiki/NTSC" title="NTSC">
            NTSC
           </a>
           television at
           <a href="/wiki/Paddington,_New_South_Wales" title="Paddington, New South Wales">
            Paddington
           </a>
           , in Sydney. This meant that Australian viewers saw the moonwalk several seconds before the rest of the world.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
            <a href="#cite_note-139">
             [137]
            </a>
           </sup>
           See also Parkes radio astronomer John Sarkissian's article,
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.parkes.atnf.csiro.au/news_events/apollo11/" rel="nofollow">
            "On Eagle's Wings: The Parkes Observatory's Support of the Apollo 11 Mission"
           </a>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eagleswings2_140-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-eagleswings2-140">
             [138]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The events surrounding the Parkes Observatory's role in relaying the live television of the moonwalk were portrayed in a slightly fictionalized Australian film comedy "
           <a href="/wiki/The_Dish" title="The Dish">
            The Dish
           </a>
           " (2000).
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       5. Better signal was supposedly received at Parkes Observatory when the Moon was on the opposite side of the planet.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           This is not supported by the detailed evidence and logs from the missions.
          </i>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eagleswings1_141-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-eagleswings1-141">
            [139]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Missing_data">
        Missing data
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Blueprint" title="Blueprint">
        Blueprints
       </a>
       and design and development drawings of the machines involved are missing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
        <a href="#cite_note-142">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bibcollier_143-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bibcollier-143">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Apollo 11 data tapes containing
       <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">
        telemetry
       </a>
       and the high-quality video (before
       <a href="/wiki/Scan_conversion" title="Scan conversion">
        scan conversion
       </a>
       from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow-scan_TV" title="Slow-scan TV">
        slow-scan TV
       </a>
       to standard TV) of the first moonwalk are also missing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-didwego_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-didwego-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tapes">
        Tapes
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_missing_tapes" title="Apollo 11 missing tapes">
        Apollo 11 missing tapes
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11C.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="705" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Apollo11C.jpg/220px-Apollo11C.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Apollo11C.jpg/330px-Apollo11C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Apollo11C.jpg/440px-Apollo11C.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11C.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Photo of the high-quality SSTV image before the scan conversion
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11D.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Apollo11D.jpg/220px-Apollo11D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Apollo11D.jpg/330px-Apollo11D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Apollo11D.jpg/440px-Apollo11D.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11D.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Photo of the degraded image after the SSTV scan conversion
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Dr. David R. Williams (NASA archivist at
       <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">
        Goddard Space Flight Center
       </a>
       ) and Apollo 11 flight director
       <a href="/wiki/Gene_Kranz" title="Gene Kranz">
        Eugene F. Kranz
       </a>
       both acknowledged that the Apollo 11 telemetry data tapes are missing. Conspiracists see this as evidence that they never existed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-didwego_45-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-didwego-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Apollo 11 telemetry tapes were different from the telemetry tapes of the other Moon landings because they contained the raw television broadcast. For technical reasons, the Apollo 11 lander carried a
       <a href="/wiki/Slow-scan_television" title="Slow-scan television">
        slow-scan television
       </a>
       (SSTV) camera (see
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_TV_camera" title="Apollo TV camera">
        Apollo TV camera
       </a>
       ). To broadcast the pictures to regular television, a
       <a href="/wiki/Scan_conversion" title="Scan conversion">
        scan conversion
       </a>
       had to be done. The
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">
        radio telescope
       </a>
       at Parkes Observatory in Australia was able to receive the telemetry from the Moon at the time of the Apollo 11 moonwalk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eagleswings2_140-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-eagleswings2-140">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Parkes had a bigger antenna than NASA's antenna in Australia at the Honeysuckle Creek Tracking Station, so it received a better picture. It also received a better picture than NASA's antenna at
       <a href="/wiki/Goldstone_Deep_Space_Communications_Complex" title="Goldstone Deep Space Communications Complex">
        Goldstone Deep Space Communications Complex
       </a>
       . This direct TV signal, along with telemetry data, was recorded onto one-inch fourteen-track analog tape at Parkes. The original SSTV transmission had better detail and contrast than the scan-converted pictures, and it is this tape that is missing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A crude, real-time scan conversion of the SSTV signal was done in Australia before it was broadcast worldwide. However, still photos of the original SSTV image are available (see photos). About fifteen minutes of it were filmed by an amateur
       <a href="/wiki/8_mm_film" title="8 mm film">
        8 mm film
       </a>
       camera and these are also available. Later Apollo missions did not use SSTV. At least some of the telemetry tapes from the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package">
        ALSEP
       </a>
       scientific experiments left on the Moon (which ran until 1977) still exist, according to Dr. Williams. Copies of those tapes have been found.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amalfi_146-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Amalfi-146">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Others are looking for the missing telemetry tapes for different reasons. The tapes contain the original and highest quality video feed from the Apollo 11 landing. Some former Apollo personnel want to find the tapes for posterity while NASA engineers looking towards future Moon missions believe the tapes may be useful for their design studies. They have found that the Apollo 11 tapes were sent for storage at the U.S. National Archives in 1970, but by 1984, all the Apollo 11 tapes had been returned to the Goddard Space Flight Center at their request. The tapes are believed to have been stored rather than re-used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Goddard was storing 35,000 new tapes per year in 1967,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       even before the Moon landings.
      </p>
      <p>
       In November 2006,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmos_(magazine)" title="Cosmos (magazine)">
        COSMOS Online
       </a>
       reported that about 100 data tapes recorded in Australia during the Apollo 11 mission had been found in a small marine science laboratory in the main physics building at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curtin_University_of_Technology" title="Curtin University of Technology">
        Curtin University of Technology
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perth,_Western_Australia" title="Perth, Western Australia">
        Perth, Australia
       </a>
       . One of the old tapes has been sent to NASA for analysis. The slow-scan television images were not on the tape.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amalfi_146-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Amalfi-146">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In July 2009, NASA indicated that it must have erased the original Apollo 11 Moon footage years ago so that it could re-use the tape. In December 2009 NASA issued a final report on the Apollo 11 telemetry tapes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Senior engineer Dick Nafzger, who was in charge of the live TV recordings during the Apollo missions, was put in charge of the restoration project. After a three-year search, the "inescapable conclusion" was that about 45 tapes (estimated 15 tapes recorded at each of the three tracking stations) of Apollo 11 video were erased and re-used, said Nafzger.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In time for the 40th anniversary of the Apollo 11 landing,
       <a href="/wiki/Lowry_Digital" title="Lowry Digital">
        Lowry Digital
       </a>
       had been tasked with restoring the surviving footage. Lowry Digital president Mike Inchalik said that, "this is by far and away the lowest quality" video the company has dealt with. Nafzger praised Lowry for restoring "crispness" to the Apollo video, which will remain in black and white and contains conservative digital enhancements. The US$230,000 restoration project took months to complete and did not include sound quality improvements. Some selections of restored footage in high-definition have been made available on the NASA website.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Blueprints">
        Blueprints
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo15LunarRover.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1644" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Apollo15LunarRover.jpg/220px-Apollo15LunarRover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Apollo15LunarRover.jpg/330px-Apollo15LunarRover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Apollo15LunarRover.jpg/440px-Apollo15LunarRover.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo15LunarRover.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Lunar Roving Vehicle flown on Apollo 15
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The website Xenophilia.com documents a hoax claim that blueprints for the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">
        Saturn V
       </a>
       rocket,
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
        Apollo Lunar Module
       </a>
       (LM),
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">
        Lunar Roving Vehicle
       </a>
       (LRV), and associated equipment are missing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xenophilia_152-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-xenophilia-152">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are some diagrams of the Lunar Module and Lunar Roving Vehicle on the NASA website and on Xenophilia.com.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xenophilia_152-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-xenophilia-152">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Grumman" title="Grumman">
        Grumman
       </a>
       appears to have destroyed most of their LM documentation,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bibcollier_143-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-bibcollier-143">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but copies of the blueprints for the Saturn V exist on
       <a href="/wiki/Microform" title="Microform">
        microfilm
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Four mission-worthy LRVs were built by
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">
        Boeing
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Three of them were carried to the Moon on Apollos 15, 16, and 17, and left there. After Apollo 18 was canceled, the other LRV was used for spare parts for the Apollos 15 to 17 missions. The 221-page operation manual for the LRV contains some detailed drawings,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although not the blueprints.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">
        Technology
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">
        Space Race
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_space_exploration_milestones,_1957%E2%80%931969" title="List of space exploration milestones, 1957–1969">
        List of space exploration milestones, 1957–1969
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Bart Sibrel cites the relative level of the United States and USSR space technology as evidence that the Moon landings could not have happened. For much of the early stages of the Space Race, the USSR was ahead of the United States, yet in the end, the USSR was never able to fly a manned craft to the Moon, let alone land one on the surface. It is argued that, because the USSR was unable to do this, the United States should have also been unable to develop the technology to do so.
      </p>
      <p>
       For example, he claims that, during the Apollo program, the USSR had five times more manned hours in space than the United States, and notes that the USSR was the first to achieve many of the early milestones in space: the first man-made
       <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">
        satellite
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">
        orbit
       </a>
       (October 1957,
       <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">
        Sputnik 1
       </a>
       );
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [Note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the first living creature in orbit (a
       <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">
        dog
       </a>
       named
       <a href="/wiki/Laika" title="Laika">
        Laika
       </a>
       , November 1957,
       <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_2" title="Sputnik 2">
        Sputnik 2
       </a>
       ); the first man in space and in orbit (
       <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">
        Yuri Gagarin
       </a>
       , April 1961,
       <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">
        Vostok 1
       </a>
       ); the first woman in space (
       <a href="/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova" title="Valentina Tereshkova">
        Valentina Tereshkova
       </a>
       , June 1963,
       <a href="/wiki/Vostok_6" title="Vostok 6">
        Vostok 6
       </a>
       ); and the first spacewalk (EVA) (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexei_Leonov" title="Alexei Leonov">
        Alexei Leonov
       </a>
       in March 1965,
       <a href="/wiki/Voskhod_2" title="Voskhod 2">
        Voskhod 2
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       However, most of the Soviet gains listed above were matched by the United States within a year, and sometimes within weeks. In 1965, the United States started to achieve many firsts (such as the first successful
       <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">
        space rendezvous
       </a>
       ), which were important steps in a mission to the Moon. Furthermore, NASA and others say that these gains by the Soviets are not as impressive as they seem; that a number of these firsts were mere stunts that did not advance the technology greatly, or at all,
       <i>
        e.g.
       </i>
       , the first woman in space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, by the time of the launch of the first manned Earth-orbiting Apollo flight (
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_7" title="Apollo 7">
        Apollo 7
       </a>
       ), the USSR had made only nine
       <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">
        spaceflights
       </a>
       (seven with one cosmonaut, one with two, one with three) compared to 16 by the United States. In terms of spacecraft hours, the USSR had 460 hours of spaceflight; the United States had 1,024 hours. In terms of astronaut/cosmonaut time, the USSR had 534 hours of manned spaceflight whereas the United States had 1,992 hours. By the time of Apollo 11, the United States had a lead much wider than that. (See
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_human_spaceflights,_1961%E2%80%931970" title="List of human spaceflights, 1961–1970">
        List of human spaceflights, 1961–1970
       </a>
       , and refer to individual flights for the length of time.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Moreover, the USSR did not develop a successful rocket capable of a manned lunar mission until the 1980s – their
       <a href="/wiki/N1_(rocket)" title="N1 (rocket)">
        N1 rocket
       </a>
       failed on all four launch attempts between 1969 and 1972.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Soviet
       <a href="/wiki/LK_(spacecraft)" title="LK (spacecraft)">
        LK
       </a>
       lunar lander was tested in unmanned low-Earth-orbit flights three times in 1970 and 1971.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths_of_NASA_personnel">
        Deaths of NASA personnel
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In a television program about the moon-landing hoax allegations,
       <a href="/wiki/Fox_Entertainment_Group" title="Fox Entertainment Group">
        Fox Entertainment Group
       </a>
       listed the deaths of ten astronauts and of two civilians related to the manned spaceflight program as having possibly been killed as part of a cover-up.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Freeman" title="Theodore Freeman">
         Theodore Freeman
        </a>
        (killed ejecting from
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-38_Talon" title="T-38 Talon">
         T-38
        </a>
        which had suffered a
        <a href="/wiki/Bird_strike" title="Bird strike">
         bird strike
        </a>
        , October 1964)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Elliot_See" title="Elliot See">
         Elliot See
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bassett" title="Charles Bassett">
         Charlie Bassett
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/1966_NASA_T-38_crash" title="1966 NASA T-38 crash">
         T-38 crash in bad weather
        </a>
        , February 1966)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">
         Virgil "Gus" Grissom
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Ed_White_(astronaut)" title="Ed White (astronaut)">
         Ed White
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Roger_B._Chaffee" title="Roger B. Chaffee">
         Roger B. Chaffee
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
         Apollo 1
        </a>
        fire, January 1967)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Edward_Givens" title="Edward Givens">
         Edward "Ed" Givens
        </a>
        (car accident, June 1967)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Clifton_Williams" title="Clifton Williams">
         Clifton "C. C." Williams
        </a>
        (killed ejecting from T-38, October 1967)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_James_Adams" title="Michael James Adams">
         Michael J. "Mike" Adams
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/North_American_X-15" title="North American X-15">
         X-15
        </a>
        crash, November 1967. The only pilot killed during the X-15 flight test program. He was a test pilot, not a NASA astronaut, but had flown the X-15 above 50 miles.)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Henry_Lawrence,_Jr." title="Robert Henry Lawrence, Jr.">
         Robert Henry Lawrence, Jr.
        </a>
        (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-104_Starfighter" title="F-104 Starfighter">
         F-104
        </a>
        crash, December 1967, shortly after being selected as a pilot with the
        <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
         United States Air Force
        </a>
        's (later canceled)
        <a href="/wiki/Manned_Orbiting_Laboratory" title="Manned Orbiting Laboratory">
         Manned Orbiting Laboratory
        </a>
        (MOL) program.)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Baron" title="Thomas Baron">
         Thomas Ronald Baron
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">
         North American Aviation
        </a>
        employee died in an automobile collision with train, April 27, 1967, six days after testifying before
        <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">
         Rep.
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Olin_E._Teague" title="Olin E. Teague">
         Olin E. Teague
        </a>
        's House Subcommittee on NASA Oversight hearing held at
        <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">
         Cape Kennedy
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
         Florida
        </a>
        , concerning the Apollo 1 fire, after which he was fired). Baron was a quality control inspector who wrote a report critical of the Apollo program and was an outspoken critic after the Apollo 1 fire. Baron and his family were killed as their car was struck by a train at a train crossing. The deaths were an accident.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-not_faked_161-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-not_faked-161">
          [158]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
         <a href="#cite_note-162">
          [159]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Brian D. Welch, a leading official in NASA's Public Affairs Office and Director of Media Services, died a few months after appearing in the media to debunk the Fox pro-Moon hoax television show cited above.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30_163-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30-163">
          [160]
         </a>
        </sup>
        He died of a heart attack at the age of 42.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
         <a href="#cite_note-164">
          [161]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Two of them, X-15 pilot Mike Adams and MOL pilot Robert Lawrence, had no connection with the civilian manned space program of which Apollo was a part. All of the deaths listed beside Welch occurred at least 20 months
       <i>
        before
       </i>
       Apollo 11 and the subsequent flights.
      </p>
      <p>
       As of February 2016, seven of the twelve
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_Astronauts#Apollo_astronauts_who_walked_on_the_Moon" title="List of Apollo Astronauts">
        Apollo astronauts who landed on the Moon
       </a>
       between 1969 and 1972 still survive, including Buzz Aldrin. Also, nine of the twelve
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_Astronauts#Apollo_astronauts_who_flew_to_the_Moon_without_landing" title="List of Apollo Astronauts">
        Apollo astronauts who flew to the Moon without landing
       </a>
       between 1968 and 1972 still survive, including
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">
        Michael Collins
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The number of deaths within the American astronaut corps during the run-up to Apollo and while the Moon landings were happening is similar to the number of deaths suffered by the Russians. During the period 1961 to 1972, at least, eight Russian serving and former cosmonauts died:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Valentin_Bondarenko" title="Valentin Bondarenko">
         Valentin Bondarenko
        </a>
        (ground training accident, March 1961)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Grigori_Nelyubov" title="Grigori Nelyubov">
         Grigori Nelyubov
        </a>
        (suicide, February 1966)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Komarov" title="Vladimir Komarov">
         Vladimir Komarov
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_1" title="Soyuz 1">
          Soyuz 1
         </a>
        </i>
        accident, April 1967)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">
         Yuri Gagarin
        </a>
        (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiG-15" title="MiG-15">
         MiG-15
        </a>
        crash, March 1968)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Belyayev" title="Pavel Belyayev">
         Pavel Belyayev
        </a>
        (complications following surgery, January 1970)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgi_Dobrovolski" title="Georgi Dobrovolski">
         Georgi Dobrovolski
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Vladislav_Volkov" title="Vladislav Volkov">
         Vladislav Volkov
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Patsayev" title="Viktor Patsayev">
         Viktor Patsayev
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_11" title="Soyuz 11">
          Soyuz 11
         </a>
        </i>
        accident, June 1971)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Also, the overall chief of their manned-spaceflight program,
       <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">
        Sergei Korolev
       </a>
       , died while undergoing surgery in January 1966.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_response">
        NASA response
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In June 1977, NASA issued a fact sheet responding to recent claims that the Apollo Moon landings had been hoaxed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
        <a href="#cite_note-165">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The fact sheet is particularly blunt and regards the idea of faking the Moon landings to be preposterous and outlandish. NASA refers to the rocks and particles collected from the Moon as being evidence of the program's legitimacy, as they claim that these rocks could not have been formed under conditions on earth. NASA also notes that all of the operations and phases of the Apollo program were closely followed and under the scrutiny of the news media, from liftoff to splashdown. NASA responds to Bill Kaysing's book,
       <i>
        We Never Went to the Moon
       </i>
       , by identifying one of his claims of fraud regarding the lack of a crater left on the Moon's surface by the landing of the lunar module, and refuting it with facts about the soil and cohesive nature of the surface of the Moon.
      </p>
      <p>
       The fact sheet was reissued on February 14, 2001, the day before Fox television's broadcast of
       <i>
        Conspiracy Theory: Did We Land on the Moon?
       </i>
       The documentary reinvigorated the public's interest in conspiracy theories and the possibility that the Moon landings were faked, which has provoked NASA to once again defend its name.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_Stanley_Kubrick_involvement">
        Alleged Stanley Kubrick involvement
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Filmmaker
       <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">
        Stanley Kubrick
       </a>
       is accused of having produced much of the footage for Apollos 11 and 12, presumably because he had just directed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (film)">
         2001: A Space Odyssey
        </a>
       </i>
       , which is partly set on the Moon and featured advanced special effects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clydelewis_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-clydelewis-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been claimed that when
       <i>
        2001
       </i>
       was in
       <a href="/wiki/Post-production" title="Post-production">
        post-production
       </a>
       in early 1968, NASA secretly approached Kubrick to direct the first three Moon landings. The launch and splashdown would be real but the spacecraft would stay in Earth orbit and fake footage broadcast as "live from the Moon." No evidence was offered for this theory, which overlooks many facts. For example,
       <i>
        2001
       </i>
       was released before the first Apollo landing and Kubrick's depiction of the Moon's surface is much different from its appearance in Apollo video, film, and photography. Kubrick did hire
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I._Ordway_III" title="Frederick I. Ordway III">
        Frederick Ordway
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Harry_Lange_(film_designer)" title="Harry Lange (film designer)">
        Harry Lange
       </a>
       , both of whom had worked for NASA and major aerospace contractors, to work with him on
       <i>
        2001
       </i>
       . Kubrick also used some 50 mm f/0.7 lenses that were left over from a batch made by
       <a href="/wiki/Carl_Zeiss_AG" title="Carl Zeiss AG">
        Zeiss
       </a>
       for NASA. However, Kubrick only got this lens for
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Barry_Lyndon" title="Barry Lyndon">
         Barry Lyndon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1975). The lens was originally a still photo lens and needed changes to be used for motion filming.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Mockumentary" title="Mockumentary">
        mockumentary
       </a>
       based on this idea,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_Side_of_the_Moon_(documentary)" title="Dark Side of the Moon (documentary)">
         Dark Side of the Moon
        </a>
       </i>
       , could have fueled the conspiracy theory. This French mockumentary, directed by William Karel, was originally aired on Arte channel in 2002 with the title
       <i>
        Opération Lune
       </i>
       . It parodies conspiracy theories with faked interviews, stories of assassinations of Stanley Kubrick's assistants by the CIA, and a variety of conspicuous mistakes, puns, and references to old movie characters, inserted through the film as clues for the viewer. Nevertheless,
       <i>
        Opération Lune
       </i>
       is still taken at face value by some conspiracy believers.
      </p>
      <p>
       An article entitled “Stanley Kubrick and the Moon Hoax” appeared on Usenet in 1995, in the newsgroup "alt.humor.best-of-usenet". One passage – on how Kubrick was supposedly coerced into the conspiracy – reads:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        NASA further leveraged their position by threatening to publicly reveal the heavy involvement of Mr. Kubrick's younger brother, Raul, with the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Communist_Party" title="American Communist Party">
         American Communist Party
        </a>
        . This would have been an intolerable embarrassment to Mr. Kubrick, especially since the release of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">
          Dr. Strangelove
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Kubrick had no such brother – the article was a spoof, complete with a giveaway sentence describing Kubrick shooting the moonwalk "on location" on the Moon. Nevertheless, the claim was taken up in earnest;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
        <a href="#cite_note-166">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Lewis" title="Clyde Lewis">
        Clyde Lewis
       </a>
       used it almost word-for-word,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clydelewis_29-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-clydelewis-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereas
       <a href="/wiki/Room_237" title="Room 237">
        Jay Weidner
       </a>
       gave the brother a more senior status within the party:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        No one knows how the powers-that-be convinced Kubrick to direct the Apollo landings. Maybe they had compromised Kubrick in some way. The fact that his brother, Raul Kubrick, was the head of the American Communist Party may have been one of the avenues pursued by the government to get Stanley to cooperate.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
         <a href="#cite_note-167">
          [164]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In July 2009, Weidner posted on his webpage "Secrets of the Shining", where he states that Kubrick's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Shining_(film)" title="The Shining (film)">
         The Shining
        </a>
       </i>
       (1980) is a veiled confession of his role in the scam project.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
        <a href="#cite_note-168">
         [165]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
        <a href="#cite_note-169">
         [166]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This thesis was subject of refutation in an article published on
       <a href="/wiki/Seeker_(media_company)" title="Seeker (media company)">
        Seeker
       </a>
       nearly half a year later.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
        <a href="#cite_note-170">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 2015 movie
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Moonwalkers_(film)" title="Moonwalkers (film)">
         Moonwalkers
        </a>
       </i>
       is a fictional account of a CIA agent's claim of Kubrick's involvement.
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 2015, a video surfaced which allegedly shows Kubrick being interviewed shortly before his 1999 death; the video purportedly shows the director confessing to T. Patrick Murray that the Apollo Moon landings had been faked.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
        <a href="#cite_note-171">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Research quickly found, however, that the video was a
       <a href="/wiki/Hoax" title="Hoax">
        hoax
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
        <a href="#cite_note-172">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_work">
        Academic work
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 2002, NASA granted US$15,000 to James Oberg for a commission to write a point-by-point rebuttal of the hoax claims. However, NASA canceled the commission later that year, after complaints that the book would dignify the accusations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30_163-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30-163">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Oberg said that he meant to finish the book.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30_163-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30-163">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
        <a href="#cite_note-173">
         [170]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In November 2002,
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jennings" title="Peter Jennings">
        Peter Jennings
       </a>
       said "NASA is going to spend a few thousand dollars trying to prove to some people that the United States did indeed land men on the Moon," and "NASA had been so rattled, [they] hired [somebody] to write a book refuting the conspiracy theorists." Oberg says that belief in the hoax theories is not the fault of the conspiracists, but rather that of teachers and people (including NASA) who should provide information to the public.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30_163-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30-163">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2004, Martin Hendry and Ken Skeldon of the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">
        University of Glasgow
       </a>
       were awarded a grant by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        UK
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/Particle_Physics_and_Astronomy_Research_Council" title="Particle Physics and Astronomy Research Council">
        Particle Physics and Astronomy Research Council
       </a>
       to investigate Moon landing conspiracy theories.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         [171]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In November 2004, they gave a lecture at the
       <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Science_Centre" title="Glasgow Science Centre">
        Glasgow Science Centre
       </a>
       where the top ten claims by conspiracists were individually addressed and refuted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
        <a href="#cite_note-175">
         [172]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="MythBusters_special">
        <i>
         MythBusters
        </i>
        special
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href='/wiki/MythBusters_(2008_season)#Episode_104_–_"NASA_Moon_Landing"' title="MythBusters (2008 season)">
        Mythbusters "NASA Moon Landing" episode
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       An episode of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/MythBusters" title="MythBusters">
         MythBusters
        </a>
       </i>
       in August 2008 was dedicated to the Moon landings. The
       <i>
        MythBusters
       </i>
       crew tested many of the conspiracists’ claims. Some of the testings were done in a NASA training facility. All of the conspiracists' claims examined on the show were labeled as having been "Busted", meaning that the conspiracists' claims were not true.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Third-party_evidence_of_Moon_landings">
        Third-party evidence of Moon landings
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Third-party_evidence_for_Apollo_Moon_landings" title="Third-party evidence for Apollo Moon landings">
        Third-party evidence for Apollo Moon landings
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Imaging_the_landing_sites">
        Imaging the landing sites
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg/350px-584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg/525px-584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg/700px-584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:584392main_M168000580LR_ap17_area.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
          Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter
         </a>
         photo of Apollo 17 landing site (click to enlarge)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_LRO_2-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-NASA_LRO-2">
           [2]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="309" data-file-width="540" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg/220px-Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg/330px-Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg/440px-Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lroc_apollo11_landing_site_20091109_zoom.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Apollo 11 landing site – "There the lunar module sits, parked just where it landed 40 years ago, as if it still really were 40 years ago and all the time since merely imaginary." –
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">
           The New York Times
          </a>
         </i>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_lro_lm_img_176-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-nyt_lro_lm_img-176">
           [173]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Moon-landing conspiracists claim that observatories and the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       should be able to photograph the landing sites. This implies that the world's major observatories (as well as the Hubble Program) are complicit in the hoax by refusing to take photos of the landing sites. Photos of the Moon have been taken by Hubble, including at least two Apollo landing sites, but the Hubble resolution limits viewing of lunar objects to sizes no smaller than 60–75 yards (55–69 meters), which is insufficient resolution to see any landing site features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
        <a href="#cite_note-177">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In April 2001, Leonard David published an article on
       <a href="/wiki/Space.com" title="Space.com">
        space.com
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
        <a href="#cite_note-178">
         [175]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apolloclempix_179-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-apolloclempix-179">
         [176]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which showed a photo taken by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clementine_mission" title="Clementine mission">
        Clementine mission
       </a>
       showing a diffuse dark spot at the site NASA says is the Apollo 15 lander. The evidence was noticed by Misha Kreslavsky, of the Department of Geological Sciences at
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">
        Brown University
       </a>
       , and Yuri Shkuratov of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_T-shaped_Radio_telescope,_second_modification" title="Ukrainian T-shaped Radio telescope, second modification">
        Kharkiv Astronomical Observatory
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
        Ukraine
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/SMART-1" title="SMART-1">
        SMART-1
       </a>
       unmanned probe sent back photos of the landing sites, according to
       <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Foing" title="Bernard Foing">
        Bernard Foing
       </a>
       , Chief Scientist of the ESA Science Program.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
        <a href="#cite_note-180">
         [177]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "Given SMART-1’s initial high orbit, however, it may prove difficult to see artifacts," said Foing in an interview on space.com.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2002, Alex R. Blackwell of the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii" title="University of Hawaii">
        University of Hawaii
       </a>
       pointed out that some photos taken by Apollo astronauts
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apolloclempix_179-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-apolloclempix-179">
         [176]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while in orbit around the Moon show the landing sites.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">
         The Daily Telegraph
        </a>
       </i>
       published a story in 2002 saying that European astronomers at the
       <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">
        Very Large Telescope
       </a>
