       (VLT) would use it to view the landing sites. According to the article, Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Martin_West" title="Richard Martin West">
        Richard West
       </a>
       said that his team would take "a high-resolution image of one of the Apollo landing sites." Marcus Allen, a conspiracist, answered that no photos of hardware on the Moon would convince him that manned landings had happened.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
        <a href="#cite_note-181">
         [178]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As the VLT is capable of resolving equivalent to the distance between the headlights of a car as seen from the Moon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
        <a href="#cite_note-182">
         [179]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it may be able to photograph some features of the landing sites. Such photos, if and when they become available, would be the first non-NASA-produced photos of the sites at that definition.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">
        Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency
       </a>
       (JAXA) launched their
       <a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">
        SELENE
       </a>
       Moon orbiter on September 14, 2007 (JST), from
       <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">
        Tanegashima Space Center
       </a>
       . SELENE orbited the Moon at about 100 kilometres (62 mi) altitude. In May 2008, JAXA reported detecting the "halo" generated by the Apollo 15 Lunar Module engine exhaust from a Terrain Camera image.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
        <a href="#cite_note-183">
         [180]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A three-dimensional reconstructed photo also matched the terrain of an Apollo 15 photo taken from the surface.
      </p>
      <p>
       On July 17, 2009, NASA released low-resolution engineering test photos of the Apollo 11, Apollo 14, Apollo 15, Apollo 16 and Apollo 17 landing sites that have been photographed by the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter as part of the process of starting its primary mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LRO1_184-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LRO1-184">
         [181]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The photos show the descent stage of the landers from each mission on the Moon’s surface. The photo of the Apollo 14 landing site also shows tracks made by an astronaut between a science experiment (ALSEP) and the lander.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LRO1_184-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-LRO1-184">
         [181]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Photos of the Apollo 12 landing site were released by NASA on September 3, 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
        <a href="#cite_note-185">
         [182]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Intrepid
       </i>
       lander descent stage, experiment package (
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package#Apollo_12" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package">
        ALSEP
       </a>
       ), Surveyor 3 spacecraft, and astronaut footpaths are all visible. While the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
        LRO
       </a>
       images have been enjoyed by the scientific community as a whole, they have not done anything to convince conspiracists that the landings happened.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lrohoaxbelievers_186-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lrohoaxbelievers-186">
         [183]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On September 1, 2009,
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       's lunar mission
       <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">
        Chandrayaan-1
       </a>
       took photos of the Apollo 15 landing site and tracks of the lunar rovers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TOI_article_187-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TOI_article-187">
         [184]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
        <a href="#cite_note-188">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">
        Indian Space Research Organisation
       </a>
       launched their unmanned lunar probe on September 8, 2008 (IST), from
       <a href="/wiki/Satish_Dhawan_Space_Centre" title="Satish Dhawan Space Centre">
        Satish Dhawan Space Centre
       </a>
       . The photos were taken by a
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperspectral_imaging" title="Hyperspectral imaging">
        hyperspectral camera
       </a>
       fitted as part of the mission's image payload.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TOI_article_187-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-TOI_article-187">
         [184]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
        China
       </a>
       's second lunar probe,
       <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_2" title="Chang'e 2">
        Chang'e 2
       </a>
       , which was launched in 2010, can photograph the lunar surface with a resolution of up to 1.3 meters (4.3 feet). It spotted traces of the Apollo landings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
        <a href="#cite_note-189">
         [186]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moon_rocks">
        Moon rocks
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moon_rocks" title="Moon rocks">
        Moon rocks
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg/220px-Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg/330px-Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg/440px-Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Genesis_Rock" title="Genesis Rock">
          Genesis Rock
         </a>
         brought back by Apollo 15 – older than any rocks on Earth
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Apollo program collected 380 kilograms (838 lb) of
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_rock" title="Moon rock">
        Moon rocks
       </a>
       during the six manned missions. Analyses by scientists worldwide all agree that these rocks came from the Moon – no published accounts in
       <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">
        peer-reviewed
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Scientific_journal" title="Scientific journal">
        scientific journals
       </a>
       exist that dispute this claim. The Apollo samples are easily distinguishable from both
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">
        meteorites
       </a>
       and Earth rocks
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greatmoonhoax_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-greatmoonhoax-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in that they show a lack of
       <a href="/wiki/Mineral_hydration" title="Mineral hydration">
        hydrous alteration
       </a>
       products, they show evidence of having undergone impact events on an airless body, and they have unique geochemical traits. Furthermore, most are more than 200 million years older than the oldest Earth rocks. The Moon rocks also share the same traits as Soviet samples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
        <a href="#cite_note-190">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Conspiracists argue that
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center#Directors" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">
        Marshall Space Flight Center Director
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">
        Wernher von Braun
       </a>
       's trip to
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">
        Antarctica
       </a>
       in 1967 (about two years before the Apollo 11 launch) was to gather
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_meteorite" title="Lunar meteorite">
        lunar meteorites
       </a>
       to be used as fake Moon rocks. Because von Braun was a former
       <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">
        SS
       </a>
       officer (though one who had been detained by the
       <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">
        Gestapo
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
        <a href="#cite_note-191">
         [188]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the documentary film
       <i>
        Did We Go?
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-didwego_45-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-didwego-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       suggests that he could have been pressured to agree to the conspiracy to protect himself from recriminations over his past. NASA said that von Braun’s mission was "to look into environmental and logistic factors that might relate to the planning of future space missions, and hardware."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
        <a href="#cite_note-192">
         [189]
        </a>
       </sup>
       NASA continues to send teams to work in Antarctica to mimic the conditions on other planets.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is now accepted by the scientific community that rocks have been blasted from both the
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Martian
       </a>
       and lunar surface during
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact events
       </a>
       , and that some of these have landed on the Earth as meteorites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
        <a href="#cite_note-193">
         [190]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
        <a href="#cite_note-194">
         [191]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the first Antarctic lunar meteorite was found in 1979, and its lunar origin was not recognized until 1982.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korotev2005_195-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Korotev2005-195">
         [192]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, lunar meteorites are so rare that it is unlikely that they could account for the 380 kilograms of Moon rocks that NASA gathered between 1969 and 1972. Only about 30 kilograms of lunar meteorites have been found on Earth thus far, despite private collectors and governmental agencies worldwide searching for more than 20 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korotev2005_195-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Korotev2005-195">
         [192]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While the Apollo missions gathered 380 kilograms of Moon rocks, the Soviet
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_16" title="Luna 16">
        Luna 16
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_20" title="Luna 20">
        Luna 20
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_24" title="Luna 24">
        Luna 24
       </a>
       robots gathered only 326 grams combined (that is, less than one-thousandth as much). Indeed, current plans for a Martian sample return would only gather about 500 grams of soil,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
        <a href="#cite_note-196">
         [193]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a recently proposed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Pole-Aitken_basin" title="South Pole-Aitken basin">
        South Pole-Aitken basin
       </a>
       robot mission would only gather about 1 kilogram of Moon rock.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
        <a href="#cite_note-197">
         [194]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
        <a href="#cite_note-198">
         [195]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
        <a href="#cite_note-199">
         [196]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If NASA had used similar robot technology, then between 300 and 2000 robot missions would have been needed to collect the current amount of Moon rocks that is held by NASA.
      </p>
      <p>
       On the makeup of the Moon rocks, Kaysing asked: "Why was there never a mention of gold, silver, diamonds or other precious metals on the moon? Wasn't this a viable consideration? Why was this fact never discussed [
       <i>
        sic
       </i>
       ] in the press or by the astronauts?"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
        <a href="#cite_note-200">
         [197]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Geologists realize that gold and silver deposits on Earth are the result of the action of hydrothermal fluids concentrating the precious metals into veins of ore. Since in 1969 water was believed to be absent on the Moon, no geologist would bother discussing the possibility of finding these on the Moon in any great amount.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Missions_tracked_by_independent_parties">
        Missions tracked by independent parties
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Third-party_evidence_for_Apollo_Moon_landings#Apollo_missions_tracked_by_independent_parties" title="Third-party evidence for Apollo Moon landings">
        Apollo missions tracked by independent parties
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Aside from NASA, a number of groups and individuals tracked the Apollo missions as they happened. On later missions, NASA released information to the public explaining where and when the spacecraft could be sighted. Their flight paths were tracked using radar and they were sighted and photographed using telescopes. Also, radio transmissions between the astronauts on the surface and in orbit were independently recorded.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Retroreflectors">
        Retroreflectors
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2373" data-file-width="2349" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Apollo 11 retroreflector, still with its protective cover
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The presence of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retroreflectors" title="Retroreflectors">
        retroreflectors
       </a>
       (mirrors used as targets for Earth-based tracking lasers) from the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_experiment" title="Lunar Laser Ranging experiment">
        Laser Ranging Retroreflector Experiment
       </a>
       (LRRR) is evidence that there were landings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
        <a href="#cite_note-201">
         [198]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Lick_Observatory" title="Lick Observatory">
        Lick Observatory
       </a>
       attempted to detect from Apollo 11's retroreflector while Armstrong and Aldrin were still on the Moon but did not succeed until August 1, 1969.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
        <a href="#cite_note-202">
         [199]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Apollo 14 astronauts deployed a retroreflector on February 5, 1971, and
       <a href="/wiki/McDonald_Observatory" title="McDonald Observatory">
        McDonald Observatory
       </a>
       detected it the same day. The Apollo 15 retroreflector was deployed on July 31, 1971, and was detected by McDonald Observatory within a few days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
        <a href="#cite_note-203">
         [200]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Smaller retroreflectors were also put on the Moon by the Russians; they were attached to the unmanned lunar rovers
       <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_1" title="Lunokhod 1">
        Lunokhod 1
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_2" title="Lunokhod 2">
        Lunokhod 2
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
        <a href="#cite_note-204">
         [201]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion">
        Public opinion
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing_conspiracy_theories_in_popular_culture" title="Moon landing conspiracy theories in popular culture">
        Moon landing conspiracy theories in popular culture
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In a 1994 poll by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">
         The Washington Post
        </a>
       </i>
       , 9% of the respondents said that it was possible that astronauts did not go to the Moon and another 5% were unsure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
        <a href="#cite_note-205">
         [202]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A 1999
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup_Poll" title="Gallup Poll">
        Gallup Poll
       </a>
       found that 6% of the Americans surveyed doubted that the Moon landings happened and that 5% of those surveyed had no opinion,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plait156_206-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-plait156-206">
         [203]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borenstein_207-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Borenstein-207">
         [204]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallup1_208-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gallup1-208">
         [205]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallup2_209-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gallup2-209">
         [206]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which roughly matches the findings of a similar 1995
       <i>
        Time/CNN
       </i>
       poll.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plait156_206-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-plait156-206">
         [203]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Officials of the Fox network said that such skepticism rose to about 20% after the February 2001 airing of their network's television special,
       <i>
        Conspiracy Theory: Did We Land on the Moon?
       </i>
       , seen by about 15 million viewers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borenstein_207-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Borenstein-207">
         [204]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This Fox special is seen as having promoted the hoax claims.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theage_210-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-theage-210">
         [207]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsweek_211-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-newsweek-211">
         [208]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A 2000 poll conducted by the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Public_Opinion_Foundation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Public Opinion Foundation (page does not exist)">
        Public Opinion Foundation
       </a>
       <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
        (
        <a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B4_%D0%9E%D0%B1%D1%89%D0%B5%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B5_%D0%9C%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5" title="ru:Фонд Общественное Мнение">
         ru
        </a>
        )
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        ФОМ
       </i>
       ) in Russia found that 28% of those surveyed did not believe that American astronauts landed on the Moon, and this percentage is roughly equal in all social-demographic groups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
        <a href="#cite_note-212">
         [209]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
        <a href="#cite_note-213">
         [210]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
        <a href="#cite_note-214">
         [211]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2009, a poll held by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Engineering_%26_Technology" title="Engineering &amp; Technology">
         Engineering &amp; Technology
        </a>
       </i>
       magazine found that 25% of those surveyed did not believe that men landed on the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
        <a href="#cite_note-215">
         [212]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another poll gives that 25% of 18- to 25-year-olds surveyed were unsure that the landings happened.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
        <a href="#cite_note-216">
         [213]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are subcultures worldwide which advocate the belief that the Moon landings were faked. By 1977 the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">
        Hare Krishna
       </a>
       magazine
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Back_to_Godhead" title="Back to Godhead">
         Back to Godhead
        </a>
       </i>
       called the landings a hoax, claiming that, since the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       is 93,000,000 miles away, and "according to
       <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">
        Hindu mythology
       </a>
       the Moon is 800,000 miles farther away than that", the Moon would be nearly 94,000,000 miles away; to travel that span in 91 hours would require a speed of more than a million miles per hour, "a patently impossible feat even by the scientists' calculations."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goswami_217-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goswami-217">
         [214]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
        <a href="#cite_note-218">
         [215]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/James_Oberg" title="James Oberg">
        James Oberg
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">
        ABC News
       </a>
       said that the conspiracy theory is taught in
       <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
        Cuban
       </a>
       schools and wherever Cuban teachers are sent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30_163-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fake_Moon_Flight'_Myth_2003,_pp._23,_30-163">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
        <a href="#cite_note-219">
         [216]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A poll conducted in the 1970s by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Information_Agency" title="United States Information Agency">
        United States Information Agency
       </a>
       in several countries in
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">
        Latin America
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">
        Asia
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        Africa
       </a>
       found that most respondents were unaware of the Moon landings, many of the others dismissed them as
       <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">
        propaganda
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">
        science fiction
       </a>
       , and many thought that it had been the
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russians
       </a>
       that landed on the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
        <a href="#cite_note-220">
         [217]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-15">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-15">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           In 1968, Clarke and Kubrick had collaborated on the film
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (film)">
             2001: A Space Odyssey
            </a>
           </i>
           , which realistically portrayed a Moon mission.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-82">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-82">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           This number includes the crews of Apollos 8,
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">
            10
           </a>
           , and
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">
            13
           </a>
           , though the latter technically only performed a fly-by. These three missions account for only six additional astronauts because James Lovell orbited the Moon twice (Apollos 8 and 13) and John Young and Gene Cernan orbited on Apollo 10 both later landed on the Moon.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-157">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-157">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           According to the 2007
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Nova_(TV_series)" title="Nova (TV series)">
             NOVA
            </a>
           </i>
           episode "
           <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nova_episodes#Season_35:_2007–2008" title="List of Nova episodes">
            Sputnik Declassified
           </a>
           ," the United States could have launched the
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Explorer_1" title="Explorer 1">
             Explorer 1
            </a>
           </i>
           probe before Sputnik, but the
           <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">
            Eisenhower
           </a>
           administration hesitated, first because they were not sure if international law meant that national borders kept going all the way into orbit (and, thus, their orbiting satellite could cause an international uproar by violating the borders of dozens of nations), and second because there was a desire to see the not yet ready
           <a href="/wiki/Project_Vanguard" title="Project Vanguard">
            Vanguard satellite program
           </a>
           , designed by American citizens, become America's first satellite rather than the Explorer program, that was mostly designed by former rocket designers from
           <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
            Nazi Germany
           </a>
           . A transcript of the appropriate section from the show is available at "
           <a class="external text" href="https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/nova/space/sputnik-impact-on-america.html" rel="nofollow">
            Sputnik's Impact on America
           </a>
           ."
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Moon%3A+A+Mutual+Reality+Art+Experience&amp;rft.place=Cincinnati%2C+OH&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Cincinnati&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F43837978&amp;rft.au=Britton%2C+Benjamin+%28Executive+Producer%29&amp;rft.au=Britton%2C+Lisa+%28Associate+Producer%29&amp;rft.au=Ranen%2C+Aron+%28Video+Documentary%29&amp;rft.au=Troy+Gerth+%28Lunar+South+Pole+Virtual+Museum%29&amp;rft.au=Derrick+Woodham+%28Lunar+South+Pole+Virtual+Museum%29&amp;rft.au=Ken+Rhee+%28Virtual+Reality+Moon+Landing%29&amp;rft.au=Bill+Mauritzen+%28Virtual+Reality+Moon+Landing%29&amp;rft.au=Chih-min+Chen+%28Virtual+Reality+Moon+Landing%29&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMoon+landing+conspiracy+theories">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          Abstract: "The Moon Project is a full-screen, Mutual Reality Internet DVD made on the event of the 30th anniversary of humankind's first moon landing. It is to bring viewers into personal contact with humanity's cultural tradition through participation in the exploration of the moon. As an historical source document and as an artistic interpretation of cultural context, the Moon project is made to connect viewers in our collective global culture."

           <q>
            NASA's Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter, or LRO, has returned its first imagery of the Apollo Moon landing sites. The pictures show the Apollo missions' lunar module descent stages sitting on the moon's surface, as long shadows from a low sun angle make the modules' locations evident.
